    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41422"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A high-quality genome assembly of quinoa provides insights into the molecular basis of salt bladder-based salinity tolerance and the exceptional nutritional value"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Cell Research 2017 27:11"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-10-10"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Chenopodium quinoa is a halophytic pseudocereal crop that is being cultivated in an ever-growing number of countries. Because quinoa is highly resistant to multiple abiotic stresses and its seed has a better nutritional value than any other major cereals, it is regarded as a future crop to ensure global food security. We generated a high-quality genome draft using an inbred line of the quinoa cultivar Real. The quinoa genome experienced one recent genome duplication about 4.3 million years ago, likely reflecting the genome fusion of two Chenopodium parents, in addition to the &#947; paleohexaploidization reported for most eudicots. The genome is highly repetitive (64.5% repeat content) and contains 54 438 protein-coding genes and 192 microRNA genes, with more than 99.3% having orthologous genes from glycophylic species. Stress tolerance in quinoa is associated with the expansion of genes involved in ion and nutrient transport, ABA homeostasis and signaling, and enhanced basal-level ABA responses. Epidermal salt bladder cells exhibit similar characteristics as trichomes, with a significantly higher expression of genes related to energy import and ABA biosynthesis compared with the leaf lamina. The quinoa genome sequence provides insights into its exceptional nutritional value and the evolution of halophytes, enabling the identification of genes involved in salinity tolerance, and providing the basis for molecular breeding in quinoa."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Chenopodium quinoa</i> is a halophytic pseudocereal crop that is being cultivated in an ever-growing number of countries. Because quinoa is highly resistant to multiple abiotic stresses and its seed has a better nutritional value than any other major cereals, it is regarded as a future crop to ensure global food security. We generated a high-quality genome draft using an inbred line of the quinoa cultivar Real. The quinoa genome experienced one recent genome duplication about 4.3 million years ago, likely reflecting the genome fusion of two <i>Chenopodium</i> parents, in addition to the Î³ paleohexaploidization reported for most eudicots. The genome is highly repetitive (64.5% repeat content) and contains 54 438 protein-coding genes and 192 microRNA genes, with more than 99.3% having orthologous genes from glycophylic species. Stress tolerance in quinoa is associated with the expansion of genes involved in ion and nutrient transport, ABA homeostasis and signaling, and enhanced basal-level ABA responses. Epidermal salt bladder cells exhibit similar characteristics as trichomes, with a significantly higher expression of genes related to energy import and ABA biosynthesis compared with the leaf lamina. The quinoa genome sequence provides insights into its exceptional nutritional value and the evolution of halophytes, enabling the identification of genes involved in salinity tolerance, and providing the basis for molecular breeding in quinoa.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>Chenopodium quinoa</i> Willd. is an annual pseudocereal crop whose cultivation is expanding globally. During the past three decades, the number of countries growing this crop increased by 10-fold (from 8 in 1980 to 75 in 2014)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bazile D, Jacobsen SE, Verniau A . The global expansion of quinoa: trends and limits. Front Plant Sci 2016; 7:622." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d13812e765">1</a></sup>, mainly because quinoa is resistant to multiple abiotic stresses and the seed has exceptional nutritional qualities<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Nowak V, Du J, Charrondiere UR . Assessment of the nutritional composition of quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.). Food Chem 2016; 193:47â54." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d13812e769">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shabala S, Bose J, Hedrich R . Salt bladders: do they matter? Trends Plant Sci 2014; 19:687â691." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13812e772">3</a></sup>. Quinoa was originally domesticated in the Andean region of South America as early as 7000 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Risi JC, Galwey NW . Chenopodium grains of the Andes: Incan crops for modern agriculture. Adv Appl Biol 1984; 10:145â216." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d13812e776">4</a></sup>, and it is adapted to the harsh climatic conditions of the Andean area, which has a wide range of altitudes, temperature and annual precipitation. It is regarded as a facultative halophyte and shows a strong resistance to drought and low temperature as well<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Adolf VI, Jacobsen S, Shabala S . Salt tolerance mechanisms in quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.). Environ Exp Botany 2013; 92:43â54." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d13812e780">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Jacobsen SE, Mujica A, Jensen CR . The resistance of quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.) to adverse abiotic factors. Food Rev Int 2003; 19:99â109." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d13812e783">6</a></sup>. The plant achieves the highest biomass when grown in the presence of 100 mM NaCl and shows only 20%<span class="stix">â¼</span>50% biomass reduction even when treated with 500 mM NaCl (sea-water level)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Hariadi Y, Marandon K, Tian Y, Jacobsen SE, Shabala S . Ionic and osmotic relations in quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.) plants grown at various salinity levels. J Exp Bot 2011; 62:185â193." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d13812e787">7</a></sup>. Compared to other grains, quinoa seeds lack gluten and have a better balanced ratio of proteins, lipids and carbohydrates, higher contents of essential amino acids and are rich in several minerals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Nowak V, Du J, Charrondiere UR . Assessment of the nutritional composition of quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.). Food Chem 2016; 193:47â54." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d13812e792">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Woolf PJ, Fu LL, Basu A . vProtein: identifying optimal amino acid complements from plant-based foods. PLoS One 2011; 6:e18836." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d13812e795">8</a></sup>. As a result, the National Aeronautics and Space Administration (NASA) of the United States considered quinoa as the optimal food source for astronauts in space<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Schlick G, Bubenheim DL . Quinoa: an emerging &quot;New&quot; crop with potential for CELSS. NASA Technical Paper. Moffett Field, 1993; 3422:1â9." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d13812e799">9</a></sup>. The United Nations Food and Agricultural Organization (FAO) listed quinoa as an important crop to ensure global food security<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bazile D, Bertero D, Nieto C, eds. State of the art report on quinoa around the world in 2013. FAO &amp; CIRAD. Rome, 2015." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13812e803">10</a></sup> and declared 2013 as the International Year of Quinoa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bazile D, Jacobsen SE, Verniau A . The global expansion of quinoa: trends and limits. Front Plant Sci 2016; 7:622." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d13812e807">1</a></sup>.</p><p>Quinoa belongs to the flowering-plant family <i>Amaranthaceae</i>, which also contains sugar beet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Dohm JC, Lange C, Holtgrawe D, et al. Palaeohexaploid ancestry for Caryophyllales inferred from extensive gene-based physical and genetic mapping of the sugar beet genome (Beta vulgaris). Plant J 2012; 70:528â540." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d13812e817">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Dohm JC, Minoche AE, Holtgrawe D, et al. The genome of the recently domesticated crop plant sugar beet (Beta vulgaris). Nature 2014; 505:546â549." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d13812e820">12</a></sup>, spinach and another pseudocereal, amaranth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Sunil M, Hariharan AK, Nayak S, et al. The draft genome and transcriptome of Amaranthus hypochondriacus: a C4 dicot producing high-lysine edible pseudo-cereal. DNA Res 2014; 21:585â602." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d13812e824">13</a></sup>. Quinoa is an allotetraploid (2n = 4Ã = 36) with an estimated genome size of approximately 1.5 Gbp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Palomino G, Hernandez LT, Torres ED . Nuclear genome size and chromosome analysis in Chenopodium quinoa and C-berlandieri subsp nuttalliae. Euphytica 2008; 164:221â230." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d13812e828">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Kolano B, McCann J, Orzechowska M, Siwinska D, Temsch E, Weiss-Schneeweiss H . Molecular and cytogenetic evidence for an allotetraploid origin of Chenopodium quinoa and C. berlandieri (Amaranthaceae). Mol Phylogenet Evol 2016; 100:109â123." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d13812e831">15</a></sup>. Considered as a facultative autogamous plant, quinoa was reported to show heterogeneity and an outcrossing rate that ranges from 0.5% to 17%<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Murphy KM, Bazile D, Kellogg J, Rahmanian M . Development of a Worldwide Consortium on evolutionary participatory breeding in quinoa. Front Plant Sci 2016; 7:608." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d13812e835">16</a></sup>. Despite having been domesticated for thousands of years, exceptional biodiversity is maintained in the current quinoa cultivars, through traditional seed exchanges of local Andean farmers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Fuentes FF, Bazile D, Bhargava A, Martinez EA . Implications of farmers' seed exchanges for on-farm conservation of quinoa, as revealed by its genetic diversity in Chile. J Agric Sci 2012; 150:702â716." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d13812e840">17</a></sup>. The lack of breeding for specific environments, the high photoperiodic sensitivity and the relatively low yield are the major factors that limit quinoa cultivation in non-native areas<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bazile D, Jacobsen SE, Verniau A . The global expansion of quinoa: trends and limits. Front Plant Sci 2016; 7:622." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d13812e844">1</a></sup>. Also, the genetic resources for this crop are limited<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Jarvis DE, Kopp OR, Jellen EN, et al. Simple sequence repeat marker development and genetic mapping in quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.). J Genet 2008; 87:39â51." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d13812e848">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Maughan PJ, Bonifacio A, Jellen EN, et al. A genetic linkage map of quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa) based on AFLP, RAPD, and SSR markers. Theor Appl Genet 2004; 109:1188â1195." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d13812e851">19</a></sup>, which greatly hinders molecular marker-assisted breeding efforts. In order to develop high-density molecular markers and assist in the breeding process, a high-quality genome assembly of quinoa is needed.</p><p>Recently, quinoa has been studied as a model to understand salt tolerance in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shabala S, Bose J, Hedrich R . Salt bladders: do they matter? Trends Plant Sci 2014; 19:687â691." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13812e858">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Bonales-Alatorre E, Shabala S, Chen ZH, Pottosin I . Reduced tonoplast fast-activating and slow-activating channel activity is essential for conferring salinity tolerance in a facultative halophyte, quinoa. Plant Physiol 2013; 162:940â952." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d13812e861">20</a></sup>. Epidermal bladder cells (EBCs), a cell structure homologous to trichomes, can be found in around 50% of all halophytes and are critical for their salt tolerance. In quinoa, the volume of EBCs is around 1 000 times of that of normal epidermal cells. EBCs are present when plants are not under salinity stress. The ion influxes of EBCs strongly increase in response to salt treatment and/or reactive oxygen species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shabala S, Bose J, Hedrich R . Salt bladders: do they matter? Trends Plant Sci 2014; 19:687â691." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13812e865">3</a></sup>. By engineering the trichome to salt bladder transition in glycophytic crop plants, salt-tolerant crops may be generated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shabala S, Bose J, Hedrich R . Salt bladders: do they matter? Trends Plant Sci 2014; 19:687â691." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13812e869">3</a></sup>. In order to use quinoa as a model halophytic plant and to study the molecular mechanism underlying salt bladder function and development, a high-quality genome draft is also important.</p><p>In this study, we generated a genome draft for <i>C. quinoa</i> using an inbred line of the quinoa Real, one of the most widely cultivated landraces in the world. We also generated high-depth transcriptome data from five representative types of quinoa tissues, and from EBCs with or without salt treatment. The expansion of gene families involved in stress response, ABA signaling and ion transport and a constitutive stress response at the transcript level were found to be correlated with the remarkable stress tolerance in quinoa. We also identified EBC-specific expression of ion transporters, H<sup>+</sup>-ATPases and sugar transporters supporting a model of polarized salt sequestration from leaf cells to EBCs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shabala S, Bose J, Hedrich R . Salt bladders: do they matter? Trends Plant Sci 2014; 19:687â691." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13812e881">3</a></sup>. The data presented here will facilitate studies to understand the molecular mechanisms of salt tolerance and help with the breeding of quinoa and the engineering of salt-tolerant crops.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Phylogeny of <i>Chenopodium quinoa</i></h3><p>Quinoa is a member of the plant family Amaranthaceae, which typically has a base chromosome number of 8â9. <i>C. quinoa</i> is believed to have been derived from the genome fusion of two related parent species from the same genus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Kolano B, McCann J, Orzechowska M, Siwinska D, Temsch E, Weiss-Schneeweiss H . Molecular and cytogenetic evidence for an allotetraploid origin of Chenopodium quinoa and C. berlandieri (Amaranthaceae). Mol Phylogenet Evol 2016; 100:109â123." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d13812e903">15</a></sup>. Amaranthaceae contains more than 2 000 known species, including some important economic plants, such as spinach (<i>Spinacia oleracea</i>), sugar beet (<i>Beta vulgaris</i>) and <i>Amaranthus hypochondriacus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Group TAP . An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot J Linean Soc 2016; 181:1â20." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d13812e915">21</a></sup>. Amaranthaceae is the largest family of the order Caryophyllales (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig1">Figure 1A</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Group TAP . An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot J Linean Soc 2016; 181:1â20." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d13812e923">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Bell CD, Soltis DE, Soltis PS . The age and diversification of the angiosperms re-revisited. Am J Bot 2010; 97:1296â1303." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d13812e926">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Moore MJ, Soltis PS, Bell CD, Burleigh JG, Soltis DE . Phylogenetic analysis of 83 plastid genes further resolves the early diversification of eudicots. Proc Natl Acad Sci USA 2010; 107:4623â4628." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d13812e929">23</a></sup>, which represents more than 6% of angiosperm species diversity and is found in all terrestrial ecosystems<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Yang Y, Moore MJ, Brockington SF, et al. Dissecting molecular evolution in the highly diverse plant clade caryophyllales using transcriptome sequencing. Mol Biol Evol 2015; 32:2001â2014." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d13812e933">24</a></sup>. Several independent lineages of C4 and CAM (crassulacean acid metabolism) plants, as well as a clade of carnivorous plants, belong to this order<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Group TAP . An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot J Linean Soc 2016; 181:1â20." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d13812e937">21</a></sup>. Despite an extraordinary diversity within Caryophyllales, genome sequences are available only for a limited number of species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Dohm JC, Minoche AE, Holtgrawe D, et al. The genome of the recently domesticated crop plant sugar beet (Beta vulgaris). Nature 2014; 505:546â549." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d13812e941">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Sunil M, Hariharan AK, Nayak S, et al. The draft genome and transcriptome of Amaranthus hypochondriacus: a C4 dicot producing high-lysine edible pseudo-cereal. DNA Res 2014; 21:585â602." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d13812e944">13</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig1">Figure 1A</a>). Caryophyllales and Rosids belong to the clade of Pentapetalae, which shares a paleohexaploid ancestor (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig1">Figure 1A</a>) and is estimated to have a crown age of 104-113 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Moore MJ, Soltis PS, Bell CD, Burleigh JG, Soltis DE . Phylogenetic analysis of 83 plastid genes further resolves the early diversification of eudicots. Proc Natl Acad Sci USA 2010; 107:4623â4628." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d13812e955">23</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/cr2017124/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fcr.2017.124/MediaObjects/41422_2017_Article_BFcr2017124_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fcr.2017.124/MediaObjects/41422_2017_Article_BFcr2017124_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="408"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Evolution and synteny analyses of the <i>Chenopodium quinoa</i> genome. <b>(A)</b> Proposed evolution scenario of <i>C. quinoa</i>. The representative species from different orders are listed. Orders are indicated by different colors and order names are listed to the right. Orange points represent the event of whole-genome duplication or genome fusion. <i>C.</i>? represents the diploid parents that give rise to the allotetraploidic <i>C. quinoa</i>. <b>(B)</b> Phylogenetic analysis using selected plant species with published genome assemblies. It is estimated that <i>C. quinoa</i> and <i>Spinacia oleracea</i> were separated about 16 million years ago (MYA). <b>(C)</b> Four-fold-degenerate analyses suggest that the genome fusion event of <i>C. quinoa</i> (<i>Cq</i>) happened recently, after the divergence from spinach (<i>So</i>; <i>S. oleracea</i>) and sugar beet (<i>Bv</i>; <i>Beta vulgaris</i>). <b>(D)</b> Triplicated and <b>(E)</b> quadruplicated syntenic blocks in the <i>C. quinoa</i> genome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/cr2017124/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>An inbred line of <i>C. quinoa</i> f. <i>real</i> that showed a low heterozygosity ratio in a pilot survey was used for genome sequencing (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S1</a>). Using a K-mer distribution analysis, we estimated that quinoa has a genome size of apporoximately 1.45 Gbp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S1</a>), which is consistent with previous estimates based on C-values<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Palomino G, Hernandez LT, Torres ED . Nuclear genome size and chromosome analysis in Chenopodium quinoa and C-berlandieri subsp nuttalliae. Euphytica 2008; 164:221â230." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1054">14</a></sup>.</p><p>We took a hybrid approach for genome assembly, combining 284 Gb of paired-end reads from HiSeq2500 (Illumina) and 50 Gb of single-molecule long reads from RS II (PacBio) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S1</a>). After filtering out low-quality bases, a total of 153 Gb of data from two small-size PCR-free libraries (<span class="stix">â¼</span>80Ã coverage), two medium-size mate pair libraries (5 and 8 kb; <span class="stix">â¼</span>30Ã coverage) and one 20-kb PacBio library (<span class="stix">â¼</span>34Ã coverage), were used for genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S1</a>). The final v1.0 assembly (Cq_real_v1.0) has a total length of 1 337 Mbp with a scaffold N50 equaling 1.16 Mbp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The assembly covers 90.2% of the nuclear genome based on our estimation. Importantly, 90% of the assembly falls into 1 087 scaffolds that are at least 423 Kbp in length (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S1</a>), with the largest scaffold being 5.4 Mbp. By utilizing the high coverage of single-molecule long reads on organelle genomes, we assembled the chloroplast genome into one single contig with a length of 152 282 bp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S2</a>). However, a similar attempt failed to generate a complete genome for the mitochondrion.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Global statistics of <i>Chenopodium quinoa</i> genome assembly and annotation</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/cr2017124/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In order to assess the quality of Cq_real_v1.0, we assembled 234 311 transcripts (&gt; 200 bp) <i>de novo</i> from 60-Gb mRNA-seq data of five different quinoa tissues; 98.9% of these could be aligned to the assembly with more than 95% identity, demonstrating that the majority of gene space was covered (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S3</a>). More than 98% of the Illumina reads from the two PCR-free libraries can be aligned back to the final assembly. After the alignment, 34 197 SNPs and 41 269 InDels were identified. 90% of the InDels had a size of less than 10 bp. That allowed us to estimate the consensus error rate as 0.0056%. Comparison to a published quinoa-genetic map containing 511 SNP (single-nucleotide polymorphism) markers, allowed 485 of our scaffolds to be anchored to 497 SNP markers by allowing one mismatch (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S3</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Maughan PJ, Smith SM, Rojas-Beltran JA, et al. Single nucleotide polymorphism identification, characterization, and linkage mapping in quinoa. Plant Genome 2012; 5:114â125." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1365">25</a></sup>. We also generated a quinoa fosmid library and pooled 10 random fosmids for high-depth single-molecule sequencing. After <i>de novo</i> assembly, we obtained 10 contigs corresponding to the 10 fosmids, all of which can be globally aligned to a specific scaffold with greater than 98% identity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S4</a>). These metrics indicate that our assembly is of high quality and has a low error rate.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome annotation</h3><p>We annotated the repeats in the genome by combining <i>in silico</i> prediction and homology searches. As much as 64.5% of the <i>C. quinoa</i> genome comprised various types of repeat sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Tab1">Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S5</a>), 85.6% of which are transposable elements (TEs). Retrotransposons comprise the majority of the TEs. LTR-type (long terminal repeat) transposons <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i>, and the DNA transposon CMC are the three most abundant transposon families, making up 33.58%, 11.69% and 3.64% of the total genome content, respectively. Simple sequence repeats (SSRs) are usually polymorphic and provide a rich source of molecular markers for breeding and genetic studies. A total of 392 764 SSRs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S6</a>), ranging from mononucleotide to hexanucleotide motifs and representing 38 distinctive motif families, were identified and annotated in the <i>C. quinoa</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S7</a>).</p><p>We performed gene prediction by combining the results obtained from <i>ab initio</i> prediction, homology searches and transcriptome assembly, after masking the repetitive sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S8</a>). A total of 54 438 protein-coding genes were identified in the <i>C. quinoa</i> genome, that had an average length of 3 548 bp and contained an average of 4.8 exons per gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Tab1">Table 1</a>). We also identified 192 micro RNAs (miRNAs), 1 310 ribosomal RNAs (rRNAs), 2 934 transfer RNAs (tRNAs) and 5 922 small nuclear RNAs (snRNAs) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Tab1">Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S9</a>). We assigned the functions to 95.6% of the protein-coding genes based on functional annotations from five different public databases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S10</a>). Remarkably, 75.4% of quinoa proteins showed a homology to the InterPro database, and 85.0% were identified in the gene ontology (GO) database (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S10</a>).</p><p>Our predicted gene set covers 93.3% of the 1 440 single-copy orthologous genes in BUSCO v2 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S11</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Simao FA, Waterhouse RM, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM . BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 2015; 31:3210â3212." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1446">26</a></sup>. More than 50% of the predicted gene models have a probabilistic confidence score of 1.0, indicating that evidence from <i>de novo</i> prediction, homology-based gene prediction and mRNA-seq data are mostly consistent (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S4A</a>; see Materials and Methods section for details). Indeed &gt; 76% of the predicted genes with a confidence score greater than 0.5 (corresponding to <span class="stix">â¼</span>95% of all genes) contain at least one conserved protein domain (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S4B</a>). Over 96% of the annotated genes had supporting mRNA-sequencing reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S4C</a>). Using 56 quinoa mRNA sequences available in the NCBI nucleotide database, we estimated the specificity and sensitivity at the exon level to be 69.74% and 43.09%, respectively. This estimation of exon specificity is consistent with the estimation of exon sensitivity (71.92%) using another set of high-expression transcripts assembled from mRNA-seq data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S12</a>). These results indicate a high accuracy of predicted gene models in Cq_real_v1.0.</p><p>We compared Cq_real_v1.0 and two recently published quinoa genome drafts. While all three assemblies have consistent estimates of the GC ratio and the repeat content, the two assemblies utilizing single-molecule long reads clearly have a better coverage and contiguity than the one using only short reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S13</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Yasui Y, Hirakawa H, Oikawa T, et al. Draft genome sequence of an inbred line of Chenopodium quinoa, an allotetraploid crop with great environmental adaptability and outstanding nutritional properties. DNA Res 2016; 23:535â546." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1472">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Jarvis DE, Ho YS, Lightfoot DJ, et al. The genome of Chenopodium quinoa. Nature 2017; 542:307â312." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1475">28</a></sup>. ASM168347v1 has a similar number of scaffolds and covers a similar number of bases as Cq_real_v1.0 (<span class="stix">â¼</span>1 325 Mbp), but has a higher scaffold of N50 length and a smaller number of N50 scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S13</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Jarvis DE, Ho YS, Lightfoot DJ, et al. The genome of Chenopodium quinoa. Nature 2017; 542:307â312." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1482">28</a></sup>. On the other hand, Cq_real_v1.0 includes 9 693 more gene models while covering approximately 98.5% (44 124 of 44 776) of the predicted genes in ASM168347v1 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S13</a>). Most of the 10 554 Cq_real_v1.0-specific gene models have supporting mRNA-seq data and 78.3% of them are expressed (RPKM &gt; 2) in at least 1 of the 11 types of quinoa samples examined (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S5</a>).</p><p>Comparative analyses of <i>C. quinoa</i> with <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Guimaraes PM, Brasileiro ACM, Morgante CV, et al. Global transcriptome analysis of two wild relatives of peanut under drought and fungi infection. BMC Genomics 2012; 13:387â387." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1502">29</a></sup>, and three other species from the <i>Amaranthaceae</i> family, <i>A. hypochondriacus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Sunil M, Hariharan AK, Nayak S, et al. The draft genome and transcriptome of Amaranthus hypochondriacus: a C4 dicot producing high-lysine edible pseudo-cereal. DNA Res 2014; 21:585â602." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1511">13</a></sup>, <i>S. oleracea</i> and <i>B. vulgaris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Dohm JC, Minoche AE, Holtgrawe D, et al. The genome of the recently domesticated crop plant sugar beet (Beta vulgaris). Nature 2014; 505:546â549." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1521">12</a></sup> indicated that the five plant species possess similar numbers of orthologous groups, with a core set of 10 538 in common (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S6</a>). Out of the 14 707 <i>C. quinoa</i> gene families, 14 492 were identified at least once in the other four plant genomes. Among the 315 quinoa-specific gene orthologous groups, 36.5% (825 genes) were predicted with an unknown function, while genes involved in auxin efflux transporter activity and the response to molecule of fungal origin were among the most significantly overrepresented (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S14</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genome evolution of quinoa</h3><p>Cytological and molecular evidence suggests that the quinoa genome is the result of genome fusion between two different parent species of <i>Chenopodium</i>, each contributing to about half of the genome size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Kolano B, McCann J, Orzechowska M, Siwinska D, Temsch E, Weiss-Schneeweiss H . Molecular and cytogenetic evidence for an allotetraploid origin of Chenopodium quinoa and C. berlandieri (Amaranthaceae). Mol Phylogenet Evol 2016; 100:109â123." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1545">15</a></sup>. Using the available genome sequences from the related species, we are able to identify the key time points in quinoa genome evolution. By examining single-copy gene families from eight sequenced plant genomes, we found that both quinoa and spinach (<i>S. oleracea</i>), belonging to a subfamily <i>Chenopodiaceae</i>, diverged some 16 million years ago, and shared a common ancestor with <i>A. hypochondriacus</i>, diverging about 25 million years ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig1">Figure 1B</a>). Caryophylales and Rosids diverged about 82 million years ago. Consistent with previous studies, our phylogenetic analyses strongly suggest that Caryophyllales represents the most basal eudicot clade (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig1">Figure 1B</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Dohm JC, Minoche AE, Holtgrawe D, et al. The genome of the recently domesticated crop plant sugar beet (Beta vulgaris). Nature 2014; 505:546â549." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1565">12</a></sup>.</p><p>We analyzed the fourth degenerate sites (4DTv) to identify the critical time points in quinoa genome evolution. We first identified 1 224 colinear blocks in the quinoa genome, which corresponds to approximately 31% (16 864/54 438) of the total gene set. Age distribution was calculated using 9 890 paralogous gene pairs of similar age after excluding local duplications. We observed a sharp peak centered on 4DTv around 0.028 indicating a recent whole-genome duplication (WGD) event (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig1">Figure 1C</a>) that likely reflects the genome fusion between two parent <i>Chenopodium</i> species. The time of fusion is about 4.3 million years ago, which is much later than the divergence between quinoa and spinach or between quinoa and sugar beet. Within the quinoa genome, we also identified colinear blocks that corresponded to three or more different genomic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig1">Figure 1D</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig1">1E</a>), which likely reflects the Î³ paleohexaploidization event observed in many eudicots. Considering that self-colinear blocks may retain a limited ancient colinearity, we used 16 481 paralogous gene pairs from the quinoa genome and calculated the <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> value (substitution per synonymous site) distribution. We observed two peaks of <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> at 0â0.2 and 1.2â1.8, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S7</a>). The first peak likely indicates the recent genome fusion event, while the second peak corresponds to the Î³ paleohexaploidization event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Tang H, Bowers JE, Wang X, Ming R, Alam M, Paterson AH . Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 2008; 320:486â488." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1596">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Van de Peer Y, Fawcett JA, Proost S, Sterck L, Vandepoele K . The flowering world: a tale of duplications. Trends Plant Sci 2009; 14:680â688." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1599">31</a></sup>. Thus, besides the genome fusion event that happened around 4.3 million years ago, no other lineage-specific WGD events were involved in quinoa genome evolution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig1">Figure 1A</a>). This is similar to what was observed for the <i>B. vulgaris</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Dohm JC, Minoche AE, Holtgrawe D, et al. The genome of the recently domesticated crop plant sugar beet (Beta vulgaris). Nature 2014; 505:546â549." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1610">12</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Quinoa as a lysine-rich pseudocereal</h3><p>As a pseudocereal, quinoa is valued for its high protein content and richness in essential amino acids (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig2">Figure 2A</a>). For example, lysine content is usually limited in cereal grains but plentiful in legumes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Young VR, Pellett PL . Plant proteins in relation to human protein and amino acid nutrition. Am J Clin Nutr 1994; 59:1203Sâ1212S." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1626">32</a></sup>. We analyzed three families of candidate seed storage proteins, including albumin, globulin and late embryo abundant (LEA) proteins. We found that the lysine content of quinoa in all three protein families was significantly higher in comparison to the other cereals, such as wheat, rice or maize, to a level that was equivalent to soybeans (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig2">Figure 2B-2D</a>). The consistently higher contents in all seed storage proteins were not observed for threonine or methionine, which share the same precursor as lysine for biosynthesis. We also observed a significantly higher usage of phenylalanine and isoleucine in all the seed storage proteins compared to other cereals (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figures S8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">S9</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">S10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Table S16</a>). Thus, the high contents of essential amino acids in quinoa are not only reflected at the level of free amino acids, but also at the level of amino acid usage in seed protein sequences.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/cr2017124/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fcr.2017.124/MediaObjects/41422_2017_Article_BFcr2017124_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fcr.2017.124/MediaObjects/41422_2017_Article_BFcr2017124_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="500" height="775"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Gene copy number expansion associated with nutrient synthesis in the quinoa genome. <b>(A)</b> Photos of mature panicles (left) and dry seeds (right) of quinoa. <b>(B</b>-<b>D)</b> The sequences of quinoa seed storage proteins contain a significantly higher proportion of lysine residues compared to other cereals. The boxplots show the distribution of lysine percentage (% Lys â number of lysine residues divided by the total length of proteins) in the sequences of indicated protein families: <b>(B)</b> albumin, <b>(C)</b> globulin and <b>(D)</b> LEA (late embryogenesis abundant). <b>(E</b>-<b>G)</b> Diagrams showing the key steps in the biosynthesis of lysine <b>(E)</b>, vitamin B6 <b>(F)</b> and folate <b>(G)</b> in plants. The arrow line indicates the direction of biochemical reactions, and enzymes catalyzing the corresponding reactions are listed as Enzyme Commission (EC) numbers next to the arrow. <b>(H)</b> Gene copy number of enzymes involved in the biosynthesis of lysine, vitamin B6 and folate in selected plant species. The enzymes that show an expansion in the quinoa genome are colored red. Species are indicated by two-letter names: <i>At</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Ats</i>, <i>Aegilops tauschii</i>, <i>Bv</i>, sugar beet (<i>Beta vulgaris</i>), <i>Cq</i>, quinoa (<i>Chenopodium quinoa</i>), <i>Gm</i>, soybean (<i>Glycine max</i>), <i>Os</i>, rice (<i>Oryza sativa</i>) and <i>Zm</i>, maize (<i>Zea mays</i>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/cr2017124/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We examined the key enzymes involved in lysine biosynthesis based on the KEGG annotation. There are seven enzymes involved in converting aspartate into lysine (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig2">Figure 2E</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig2">2H</a>). The copy numbers of two downstream enzymes, diaminopimelate aminotransferase (DAPAT; EC 2.6.1.83) and diaminopimelate epimerase (DAPE; EC 5.1.1.7), are higher than those in several cereals or sugar beet (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig2">Figure 2H</a>).</p><p>Quinoa has also been reported to contain high levels of vitamin B and vitamin E<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Nowak V, Du J, Charrondiere UR . Assessment of the nutritional composition of quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.). Food Chem 2016; 193:47." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1761">33</a></sup>. Thus, we examined the key enzymes involved in vitamin biosynthesis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig2">Figure 2F</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig2">2G</a>). One of the enzymes involved in vitamin B6 biosynthesis (pyridoxal 5â²-phosphate synthase; EC 4.3.3.6) and two enzymes that generate dihydrofolate (dihydrofolate synthase, EC 6.3.2.12; tetrahydrofolate synthase, EC 6.3.2.17) have higher gene copy numbers in quinoa than in other plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig2">Figure 2H</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Enhanced ABA signaling and biosynthesis</h3><p>In order to understand quinoa's remarkable tolerance to salinity, drought and low temperature, we examined ABA-related genes in quinoa. Through BLAST-conserved domain search and manual curation, we identified the orthologs involved in ABA biosynthesis, transport and perception. We found that the key factors in each category were expanded in the quinoa genome. ABA biosynthesis in plants starts from the precursor isopentenyl diphosphate (IPP) in the plastid and proceeds through the carotenoid pathway (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig3">Figure 3A</a>). The step catalyzed by NCEDs (9-<i>cis</i>-epoxycarotenoid dioxygenases), which cleave neoxanthin and produce the C<sub>15</sub> intermediate xanthoxin, is considered to be the rate-limiting step for ABA biosynthesis. Quinoa contains 11 NCEDs, while other Caryophyllales plant species contain 4-5 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig3">Figure 3B</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Table S17</a>). We also identified the expansion of NSY (neoxanthin synthase) and ABA4, which are involved in converting violaxanthin into neoxanthin, as well as the expansion of short-chain dehydrogenases/reductases (SDRs), which are candidate enzymes for converting xanthoxin into ABA aldehyde in the cytosol. The numbers of these genes are roughly two-fold those in other diploid plants, suggesting that the duplicated ABA <i>de novo</i> synthesis genes were retained after genome fusion in quinoa.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/cr2017124/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fcr.2017.124/MediaObjects/41422_2017_Article_BFcr2017124_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fcr.2017.124/MediaObjects/41422_2017_Article_BFcr2017124_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="303"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Genes involved in ABA homeostasis and perception are expanded in quinoa. <b>(A)</b> Diagram of the ABA biosynthetic pathway. The arrows indicate the direction of biochemical reactions. Enzymes that catalyze the reaction are indicated next to the arrow: ZEP, zeaxanthin epoxidase; VDE, violaxanthin de-epoxidase; ABA4, abscisic acid-deficient 4; NSY, neoxanthin synthase; NXD1, neoxanthin-deficient 1; NCED, 9-<i>cis</i>-epoxycarotenoid dioxygenases; SDR, short-chain dehydrogenases/reductases; AAO, aldehyde oxidase. <b>(B)</b> Copy number of genes involved in ABA biosynthesis, transport and perception in quinoa and seven other plant species. Red numbers indicate gene copy numbers that are higher in quinoa. The phylogenetic relationship of these species is indicated at the top of the graph, inferred from single-copy orthologous gene sets. Genes involved in ABA biosynthesis, transport and perception are shaded in orange, yellow and pink color, respectively. <b>(C)</b> Heatmap showing the transcript level of ABA-responsive genes across different quinoa tissues. Functional characterization is based on the GO (gene ontology) annotation. In total, 108 genes in quinoa genome were assigned as a âresponse to ABAâ, and 92 of those (85%) are expressed in at least one of the seven types of tissues. Tissues are indicated in short names: Sl, seedlings; L â B, leaf without bladders; L + B, leaf with bladders; B, bladder cells; St, stem; In, inflorescence; YS, young seeds. Species are indicated by two-letter names: <i>Ah</i>, <i>Amaranthus hypochondriacus</i>; <i>At</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>; <i>Bv</i>, sugar beet (<i>Beta vulgaris</i>); <i>Cq</i>, quinoa (<i>Chenopodium quinoa</i>); <i>Sl</i>, tomato (<i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>); <i>So</i>, spinach (<i>Spinacia oleracea L.</i>); <i>St</i>, potato (<i>Solanum tuberosum</i>); <i>Vv</i>, grape (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/cr2017124/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We also observed the expansion in the ABA receptor PYL family (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig3">Figure 3B</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Table S17</a>). Quinoa contains 22 PYL genes, while the other Caryophyllales contain at most 10. This number is also higher than potato, which contains 17 PYLs. Furthermore, quinoa contains a higher number of ABCGs, the group of ABC transporters that is utilized for ABA transportation in plant cells. These results suggest that the enhanced regulation of ABA homeostasis and signaling may contribute to abiotic stress tolerance in quinoa.</p><p>We examined the expression of ABA-responsive genes in different quinoa tissues. Among the 108 genes that are assigned the GO term âresponse to ABA stimulusâ, 81% of them have a RPKM of at least three in any one of the seven tissues examined (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig3">Figure 3C</a>) and 42% of them have a RPKM value of over 20 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig3">Figure 3C</a>), indicating that quinoa has a high basal level of ABA response.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Transcriptome analyses of epidermal bladder cells</h3><p>Quinoa uses EBCs to sequester salts from metabolically active cells<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Conant GC, Wolfe KH . GenomeVx: simple web-based creation of editable circular chromosome maps. Bioinformatics 2008; 24:861â862." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1913">34</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig4">Figure 4A</a>). EBCs are observed in <span class="stix">â¼</span>50% of halophytic plants; so they are a common tissue utilized by salinity-tolerant plants to cope with salt. We thus performed transcriptome sequencing on bladder cells under salt-treated and non-treated conditions. Clustering analyses clearly showed that bladder cells exhibit a unique transcriptome profile among the different types of plant tissues tested, with a remote similarity to leaf cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S13</a>). Compared to a leaf without bladders, we identified 8 148 differentially expressed genes in bladder cells, consistent with EBCs being specialized cells for salt sequestration (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S14</a>). Among them, genes involved in the abiotic stress response, cell wall and suberin synthesis are enriched in upregulated genes, while genes involved in photosynthesis and genes encoding membrane proteins, especially chloroplast proteins, are enriched in downregulated genes, consistent with bladder cells being anabolically inactive salt sequesters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S18</a>). Considering the presumed high salt concentration (up to 1 M) in the vacuole of bladder cells and a pronounced salt gradient between the vacuole and cytoplasm, it has been proposed that a package of ion transporters is required to actively establish and maintain the gradient<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shabala S, Bose J, Hedrich R . Salt bladders: do they matter? Trends Plant Sci 2014; 19:687â691." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1930">3</a></sup>. Among the genes that are more highly expressed in the bladder cells, we did identify a suite of ion transporters, including the anion transporters SLAH, NRT and ClC, the cation transporters NHX1 and HKT1 as well as the vacuolar and plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup>-ATPases, which generate proton gradients across the plasma and the vacuolar membranes to drive ion transport (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S15</a>). In addition to these ion transporters, the transcript level of more than 10 sugar transporters is higher in the bladder cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S15</a>). Considering the low photosynthetic activity of bladder cells, they are probably involved in transporting sugar into bladder cells to support their energy and nutrient needs. These results are consistent with the previous reports of EBCs maintaining highly negative membrane potentials (around â120 mV)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shabala S, Bose J, Hedrich R . Salt bladders: do they matter? Trends Plant Sci 2014; 19:687â691." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13812e1942">3</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/cr2017124/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fcr.2017.124/MediaObjects/41422_2017_Article_BFcr2017124_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fcr.2017.124/MediaObjects/41422_2017_Article_BFcr2017124_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="540"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Prominent transporter and ABA biosynthesis activity in the epidermal bladder cells. <b>(A)</b> Photos of quinoa leaves and salt bladders. Left: top view of a 7-week-old quinoa plant. Right top: abaxial side of the fourth leaf of a 7-week-old quinoa plant; right bottom, epidermal bladder cells under a stereoscopic microscope, which are present at both the adaxial and abaxial leaf surfaces. <b>(B)</b> Transcript levels of ABA synthesis pathway genes in the leaf and the bladder cells. Only the genes that have a RPKM value higher than 1 in at least one tissue were presented. <b>(C)</b> Heatmap of the transcript level of genes involved in ABA transport and perception in the leaf and the bladder cells. <b>(D)</b> Copy number of genes that encode specific ion transporters, hemoglobin and sugar transporters mediating growth and stress response in quinoa and other plant species. Red numbers indicate gene copy numbers that are higher in quinoa. The phylogenetic relationship of these species is indicated at the top of the graph, inferred from orthologous gene sets. Species are indicated by two-letter names: <i>Ah</i>, <i>Amaranthus hypochondriacus</i>; <i>At</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Bv</i>, sugar beet (<i>Beta vulgaris</i>), <i>Cq</i>, quinoa (<i>Chenopodium quinoa</i>), <i>Sl</i>, tomato (<i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>), <i>So</i>, spinach (<i>Spinacia oleracea L.</i>), <i>St</i>, potato (<i>Solanum tuberosum</i>), <i>Vv</i>, grape (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/cr2017124/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Enhanced transporter activity was not only detected at the transcript level but also at the gene level. Compared to other eudicot species, quinoa processes higher numbers of sodium-proton exchangers, HKTs (transporters for sodium and potassium) and SLAHs (Cl-permeable anion channels) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig4">Figure 4D</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S19</a>). The number of plasma membrane ATPases and sugar transporters also almost doubled in quinoa (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig4">Figure 4D</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S19</a>), suggesting that during growth in saline environments, quinoa has evolved an elaborate system to maintain ion homeostasis throughout the plant.</p><p>After salt treatment, we identified 180 and 525 differentially expressed genes in leaf and bladder cells, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S14</a>). Only 25 genes were shared between the two groups, suggesting that leaf cells and bladder cells respond to salt treatment differently (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S14</a>). In the DEGs identified in bladder cells from salt-treated plants, genes involved in suberin and cutin biosynthesis are significantly enriched (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S18</a>), suggesting that the wax content increases in response to elevated cellular salt concentrations. Interestingly, we found that one truncated hemoglobin (trHB) gene orthologous to the Arabidopsis <i>GLB3</i> was upregulated in EBCs after salt treatment (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Table S20</a>). It also had a higher expression level in EBCs than in leaves (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S15</a>). The plant trHB was shown to have a moderate oxygen affinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Hoy JA, Hargrove MS . The structure and function of plant hemoglobins. Plant Physiol Biochem 2008; 46:371â379." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2064">35</a></sup>, so that it may be involved in promoting oxygen diffusion in the EBC. The family of hemoglobin genes also expanded in quinoa, which has eight members, compared to 1-3 in other plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig4">Figure 4D</a>). The majority of this family (7 out of 8) are orthologous to Arabidopsis HB1 or HB2, which are involved in nitric oxide (NO) metabolism (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S16</a>), suggesting that NO signaling may also be important for stress tolerance in quinoa.</p><p>Surprisingly, we found that the pathway of ABA <i>de novo</i> synthesis was upregulated in the bladder cells. The transcript levels of two NCED genes (CCG005786.3 and CCG042102.1), which are predicted to encode the rate-limiting enzyme for ABA biosynthesis, are 4-6-fold higher than in the leaves (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig4">Figure 4B</a>). Some of the SDR genes that are homologous to Arabidopsis ABA2/SDR1 are more than 1 000-fold higher in bladder cells than in leaf cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig4">Figure 4B</a>). Furthermore, in the bladder cells, we observed an elevated expression of ABA transporters, including ABCG40 (PDR) and ABCG25, as well as the majority of the PYL family of ABA receptor proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig4">Figure 4C</a>). These data suggest that bladder cells might maintain a high level of ABA homeostasis.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Despite a long history in studying the mechanisms of plant salinity tolerance, the engineering of salinity-tolerant crops remains very challenging. Salinity tolerance is a complex trait associated with multiple subtraits (e.g., ion homeostasis, osmotic balance and reactive oxygen species regulation), each having a complex genetic basis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Zhu JK . Plant salt tolerance. Trends Plant Sci 2001; 6:66â71." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2098">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Shabala S . Learning from halophytes: physiological basis and strategies to improve abiotic stress tolerance in crops. Ann Bot 2013; 112:1209â1221." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2101">37</a></sup>. That makes traditional breeding for salt tolerance very difficult, if not impossible. Even with our understanding of a part of the underlying molecular pathways of the plant salt stress response and the identification of tens of quantitative trait loci associated with stress tolerance, the manipulation of a single or a limited number of genes has proved to be so far inadequate to create salinity-tolerant crops. This difficult situation can be alleviated in two ways. First, many of the glycophytic crops have close relatives in nature that are halophytes, and understanding the evolutionary trajectory of these species may shed light on how to engineer these crops. On the other hand, some of the minor crops usually cultivated in marginal areas are already salinity tolerant. Engineering these crops for a better yield could be an easier alternative than engineering major crops to be salt tolerant. Quinoa is a perfect candidate for both aspects and the genome assembly reported in this study could greatly facilitate the research in either direction.</p><p>The quinoa genome has a high content of repeat sequences, making up 64.5% of the genome, more than half of which are retrotransposons. The transposon amplification possibly happened after the divergence between <i>Amaranthus</i> and <i>Chenopodium</i> but before the genome fusion, as the genome size of quinoa fits the additive value of the A and B candidate parent genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Kolano B, McCann J, Orzechowska M, Siwinska D, Temsch E, Weiss-Schneeweiss H . Molecular and cytogenetic evidence for an allotetraploid origin of Chenopodium quinoa and C. berlandieri (Amaranthaceae). Mol Phylogenet Evol 2016; 100:109â123." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2114">15</a></sup>.</p><p>We identified 54 438 protein-coding genes and functionally annotated 95.6% of them based on sequence homology. Quinoa genes belong to 14 707 orthologous groups with only 2.1% of them not identified in either <i>Arabidopsis</i> or three other <i>Amaranthaceae</i> species. Our analyses of the annotated quinoa genes indicated that enhanced transporter activities and increased ABA synthesis, transport and signaling may be critical for quinoa as a halophyte.
