Considering the independent evolution of halophytes in multiple plant lineages, it seems more likely that during evolution, plants found ways to better utilize their stress-fighting toolset than inventing new genes each time. This hypothesis is also supported by genomic and genetic studies in the salt crucifers <i>Eutrema salsugineum</i> and <i>Eutrema parvulum</i>, where an elevated basal level of stress responsiveness was found to be important in that knocking out the key players such as SOS1 eliminates the stress-tolerance ability<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Oh DH, Leidi E, Zhang Q, et al. Loss of halophytism by interference with SOS1 expression. Plant Physiol 2009; 151:210â222." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2133">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Wu HJ, Zhang Z, Wang JY, et al. Insights into salt tolerance from the genome of Thellungiella salsuginea. Proc Natl Acad Sci USA 2012; 109:12219â12224." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2136">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Oh DH, Dassanayake M, Haas JS, et al. Genome structures and halophyte-specific gene expression of the extremophile Thellungiella parvula in comparison with Thellungiella salsuginea (Thellungiella halophila) and Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol 2010; 154:1040â1052." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2139">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Dassanayake M, Oh D, Haas JS, et al. The genome of the extremophile crucifer Thellungiella parvula. Nat Genet 2011; 43:913." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2142">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Yang R, Jarvis DE, Chen H, et al. The reference genome of the halophytic plant Eutrema salsugineum. Front Plant Sci 2013; 4:46." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2145">42</a></sup>. Many of the ion transporters and ABA-related genes were expanded close to two-fold in quinoa, possibly a result of gene retention after genome duplication. This supports the notion that the WGD event is important for the evolution of salinity-tolerance traits in quinoa. However, polyploidy could increase or decrease salt tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Cheeseman JM . The evolution of halophytes, glycophytes and crops, and its implications for food security under saline conditions. New Phytol 2015; 206:557â570." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2150">43</a></sup>. To understand the genomic changes associated with the evolution of salinity tolerance, it will be interesting to compare quinoa to its diploid-relative species in the future.</p><p>The EBC is found in about half of the halophytes, while in the other half, salt is accumulated in metabolically inactive succulent tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shabala S, Bose J, Hedrich R . Salt bladders: do they matter? Trends Plant Sci 2014; 19:687â691." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2157">3</a></sup>. Our transcriptome analyses on this special cell type confirmed that it is a photosynthetically inactive tissue and has a strong activity in ion transportation, cell wall and wax synthesis. The latter may be used to generate a strong mechanical support and to prevent water loss. While the changes of the EBC transcriptome in response to salt treatment are consistent with the role of EBC in salt sequestration, the relatively small number of DEGs identified and the insignificant changes in the transcript level of most transporter genes suggest that bladder cells are constitutively active in salt sequestration (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Figure S15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Table S18</a>). We suggest that the regulation of ion transport in the bladder cell response to salt treatment may mainly occur at the protein level, e.g., through protein phosphorylation. Phosphorylation and dephosphorylation have been demonstrated to provide an on/off switch to the SLAC/SLAH anion channel, AKT Shaker-type K<sup>+</sup> channels as well as SOS and HAK transporters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Hedrich R . Ion channels in plants. Physiol Rev 2012; 92:1777â1811." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2169">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Scherzer S, Bohm J, Krol E, et al. Calcium sensor kinase activates potassium uptake systems in gland cells of Venus flytraps. Proc Natl Acad Sci USA 2015; 112:7309â7314." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2172">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Bertorello AM, Zhu JK . SIK1/SOS2 networks: decoding sodium signals via calcium-responsive protein kinase pathways. Pflugers Arch 2009; 458:613â619." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2175">46</a></sup>. The same transcriptome analyses also suggest that high ABA levels are required to maintain the cellular response to high osmotic stress within the bladder cell and ABA transporters are used for importing ABA from outside. Alternatively, bladder cells may function as an ABA-producing factory and export ABA to other parts of the plant for an elevated systematic response to abiotic stresses.</p><p>Overall, the greater part of the sequence data has allowed us to modify the previously suggested model of salt accumulation in EBCs that is essential for plant adaptation to saline conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shabala S, Bose J, Hedrich R . Salt bladders: do they matter? Trends Plant Sci 2014; 19:687â691." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2183">3</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#Fig5">Figure 5</a>). Toxic Na<sup>+</sup> and Cl<sup>â</sup> ions are expelled from the leaf mesophyll via SOS1 and SLAH, respectively, pass the stalk cell and are then loaded into EBC via HKT and NRT transporters, where they are sequestered in vacuoles by NHX and CLC. These transport processes are energized by plasma- and tonoplast-based H<sup>+</sup> pumps. GLUT transporters enable the unidirectional movement of monosaccharides from the mesophyll into EBC to fuel these pumps, and hemoglobin (HB) facilitates oxygen diffusion into EBC to be used for oxidative phosphorylation and ATP production.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/cr2017124/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fcr.2017.124/MediaObjects/41422_2017_Article_BFcr2017124_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fcr.2017.124/MediaObjects/41422_2017_Article_BFcr2017124_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="600" height="665"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>A proposed model for salt accumulation in bladder cells. Monosaccharides are transported from mesophyll cells to bladder cells by the GLUTs, and enter the TCA cycles for producing ATP. Hemoglobin (HB) helps with oxygen diffusion in stalk and bladder cells, and the oxygen is used for oxidative phosphorylation, which produces ATP. Vacuolar ATPase (V-ATPase) and plasma membrane H<sup>+</sup> ATPase generate the proton gradients and membrane potential that are necessary for Na<sup>+</sup> and Cl<sup>â</sup> transport from the leaf to bladder cells, and then from the cytoplasm to the vacuole of bladder cells via Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> exchanger and chloride transporters, respectively. The ratio of cell sizes drawn in the diagram does not reflect the actual ratios. Stalk cells are represented in dashed lines because no transcriptome data are currently available for this type of cells.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/cr2017124/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In future, it will be critical for quinoa research to develop transformation methods to facilitate gene manipulation. Our genome assembly will serve as a good starting point to access genetic diversities within the species, to guide targeted gene modification and for comparative genomics studies in Caryophyllales and halophytes.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Materials and Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Materials and Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Plant materials and growth conditions</h3><p><i>C. quinoa</i> f. <i>real</i> seeds were obtained from Bolivia and cultivated in the lab for four consecutive generations through single-seed descent. For genome sequencing, seeds from a single quinoa plant were germinated on the MS medium and grown in 1 L plastic pots using the ingredients described earlier<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Hariadi Y, Marandon K, Tian Y, Jacobsen SE, Shabala S . Ionic and osmotic relations in quinoa (Chenopodium quinoa Willd.) plants grown at various salinity levels. J Exp Bot 2011; 62:185â193." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2245">7</a></sup>.</p><p>For mRNA sequencing, inbred quinoa plants were grown in a controlled greenhouse at a temperature between 19 Â°C and 26 Â°C and an average humidity of <span class="stix">â¼</span>65%. Dry seeds, 1-week-old seedlings and inflorescences, leaves and stems from 6-week-old plants were collected and flash frozen in liquid nitrogen. For mRNA sequencing in EBCs, <i>C. quinoa</i> (cv.5020) plants were cultivated in a climate chamber (12-h daylight, 50% rH, 20 Â°C) using a common potting soil. After 4 weeks, NaCl treatment was started. NaCl was added with the irrigation water for the salt-treated plants. Tap water was used for the control plants. The NaCl concentration in the irrigation water was increased stepwise up to 100 mM to prevent osmotic shock of root tissues. Plants were watered every second day. After 5 weeks of salt treatment, when the salt stress was visible by reduced growth of the plants, the plant tissue was harvested for RNA extraction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">DNA extraction, library construction and sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA was extracted using the Plant DNeasy Maxi Kit (Qiagen). For the two small-size libraries, genomic DNA was fragmented in a Covaris S220 and separated on a SAGE-ELF (Sage Science). Fractions from the SAGE-ELF that have an average size of approximately 380 bp and an average size of around 450 bp were used for PCR-free library construction using the TruSeq Nano DNA Library Preparation Kit (Illumina). The two medium-size libraries (5 kbp and 8 kbp) were prepared using the GS FLX Titanium Kit (Roche) following standard protocols. All the Illumina libraries were then sequenced on a HiSeq2500 at Core Facility for Genomics of Shanghai Center for Plant Stress Biology (PSC). The 20-kb PacBio library was prepared and sequenced at Tianjin Biochip Corporation, following the manufacturer's standard protocols.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Genome assembly and assessment</h3><p>In summary, three early assembly versions for the quinoa genome were generated using Illumina reads (v0.1) and PacBio reads (v0.2 and v0.3) separately. Then, the three assemblies were merged using the HABOT2 (hybrid assembly of third-generation sequencing 2; <a href="https://github.com/asarum/HABOT2">https://github.com/asarum/HABOT2</a>) software (1gene Corp., Hangzhou, China). A final round of scaffolding and gap filling was performed using Illumina reads to obtain Cq_real_v1.0. A more detailed protocol can be found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Data S1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Gene prediction, annotation and gene model assessment</h3><p>We first annotated the repeats in Cq_real_v1.0 by combining <i>de novo</i> prediction and homology searches using RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Tarailo-Graovac M, Chen N . Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr Protoc Bioinformatics 2009; Chapter 4:Unit 4.10." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2292">47</a></sup>. Then, the repetitive sequences were masked and three approaches were used for gene prediction: <i>ab initio</i> prediction, homology search and transcriptome-assisted gene prediction. Gene models derived from the three approaches were integrated to generate the final list of gene models using GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Elsik CG, Mackey AJ, Reese JT, Milshina NV, Roos DS, Weinstock GM . Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol 2007; 8:R13." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2299">48</a></sup>. A detailed description can be found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Data S1</a>.</p><p>We assessed the gene model in Cq_real_v1.0 for its completeness, sensitivity and specificity. Completeness was assessed by comparing the predicted protein sequences to the Pfam 31.0 database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Finn RD, Coggill P, Eberhardt RY, et al. The Pfam protein families database: towards a more sustainable future. Nucleic Acids Res 2016; 44:D279âD285." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2309">49</a></sup> and to BUSCO v2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Simao FA, Waterhouse RM, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM . BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 2015; 31:3210â3212." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2313">26</a></sup>. Then, the sensitivity and specificity of the gene, exon and nucleotide level were evaluated using Eval v2.2.8<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Keibler E, Brent MR . Eval: a software package for analysis of genome annotations. BMC Bioinformatics 2003; 4:50." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2317">50</a></sup> by using mRNA sequences retrieved from the NCBI nucleotide database or high-expression genes assembled from mRNA-seq data. A detailed description can be found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/cr2017124#MOESM26">Supplementary information, Data S1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>OrthoFinder was used to determine the genes that were orthologous among seven species (<i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. quinoa</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>A. hypochondriacus</i>, <i>B. vulgaris</i> and <i>S. oleracea</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Emms DM, Kelly S . OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol 2015; 16:157." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2355">51</a></sup>. All-versus-all BLASTP with an E-value cutoff of 1eâ05 was performed and orthologous genes were clustered using OrthoFinder. Single-copy orthologous genes were extracted from the clustering results using an in-house script. MAFFT v7 was used to perform multiple-sequence alignment for each group of single-copy orthologous genes with default parameters, and transform the protein sequence alignments into nucleotide sequence alignments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Katoh K, Standley DM . MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol Biol Evol 2013; 30:772â780." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2359">52</a></sup>. Conserved blocks were extracted from the multiple-sequence alignment results using Gblocks 0.91<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Castresana J . Selection of conserved blocks from multiple alignments for their use in phylogenetic analysis. Mol Biol Evol 2000; 17:540â552." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2363">53</a></sup> (a minimum number of sequences for a conserved position: 9; a minimum number of sequences for a flank position: 14; a maximum number of contiguous nonconserved positions: 8 and a minimum length of a block: 10), and then concatenated to one supergene for phylogenetic analysis. PhyML3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Guindon S, Dufayard JF, Lefort V, Anisimova M, Hordijk W, Gascuel O . New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst Biol 2010; 59:307â321." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2367">54</a></sup> was used to build the species phylogenetic tree with the parameters: -m HKY85-rates gamma-b-4, and PAML v4.8a<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Yang Z . PAML: a program package for phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Comput Appl Biosci 1997; 13:555â556." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2372">55</a></sup> was used to compute the mean substitution rates and to estimate the species divergence time.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Colinearity analyses</h3><p>For gene colinearity analyses, all-versus-all BLASTP searches (with an E-value cutoff of 1 Ã 10<sup>â5</sup>) were performed to identify paralogous or orthologous gene pairs. Colinear blocks containing at least five genes were identified using MCScanX with the parameters: -s 3 -m 5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Wang Y, Tang H, Debarry JD, et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res 2012; 40:e49." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2386">56</a></sup>. The <i>K</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> value of paralogous or orthologous gene pairs was calculated using an in-house Perl script. Circos software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Krzywinski M, Schein J, Birol I, et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res 2009; 19:1639â1645." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2399">57</a></sup> was used to illustrate the position relationships between syntenic blocks on the assembled scaffolds.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Gene copy analyses</h3><p>A well-characterized <i>Arabidopsis</i> member of each protein family was used as a seed to search for similar protein sequences in quinoa and other plant species using BLASTP. Candidates with an E-value smaller than 10eâ5 were then searched against the Pfam 31.0 database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Finn RD, Coggill P, Eberhardt RY, et al. The Pfam protein families database: towards a more sustainable future. Nucleic Acids Res 2016; 44:D279âD285." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2414">49</a></sup> to ensure that all the major conserved domains that are present in the seed are also present in the candidates. The candidate protein sequences were then manually examined to exclude the outliers that have very short protein sequences relative to others. The gene copy number in each species is manually counted.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">RNA extraction and library preparation</h3><p>RNA from salt bladders was isolated using the NucleoSpin RNA Plant Kit (Macherey-Nagel) according to the manual with the following modifications. Young leaves having mostly intact, turgescent bladders were dipped into liquid nitrogen. The bladders were scratched off from the leaves with a small spatula. For each sample, bladders of six young leaves from two individual plants were collected in 500 Î¼l of lysis buffer RA1 (containing 1:100 TCEP) and mixed vigorously for 20 s to lyse the cells. The lysate was transferred to NucleoSpin Filters and centrifuged for 1 min at 11 000Ã <i>g</i>. The filtrate was mixed with 500 Î¼l of ethanol (70%) and transferred in two steps to a NucleoSpin RNA plant Column and centrifuged for 1 min at 11 000Ã <i>g</i>. The washing steps were performed following the manufacturer's instructions. RNA was eluted in 33 Î¼l of RNase-free water that was incubated on the membrane for 1 min, centrifuged and incubated for a second time on the membrane. RNA from leaves as well as leaves without bladders was isolated using the E.Z.N.A plant RNA Kit (OMEGA Bio-Tek) following the manufacturer's instructions. The leaves were of the same age as those from which the bladders were removed. The rest of the latter was used for the âleaves without bladderâ samples. Strand-specific mRNA libraries were prepared at Core Facility for Genomics at Shanghai Center for Plant Stress Biology (PSC) using NEBNext Ultra Directional RNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina (New England BioLabs, Cat No. E7420). The libraries were then sequenced on a HiSeq2500 (Illumina) using the paired-end 125-bp sequencing mode.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Transcriptome analyses</h3><p>Raw sequencing reads were filtered using a quality cutoff of 30 while removing the adapter sequences. The clean reads were then mapped to the Cq_real_v1.0 assembly using subread-align (v1.5.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Liao Y, Smyth GK, Shi W . The Subread aligner: fast, accurate and scalable read mapping by seed-and-vote. Nucleic Acids Res 2013; 41:e108." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2440">58</a></sup>. Only uniquely mapped paired-end reads were retained for read counting against the annotated gene models using featureCounts (v1.5.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Liao Y, Smyth GK, Shi W . featureCounts: an efficient general purpose program for assigning sequence reads to genomic features. Bioinformatics 2014; 30:923â930." href="/articles/cr2017124#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d13812e2444">59</a></sup>. The edgeR package (v3.14.0) was then used to identify differentially expressed genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Data availability</h3><p>The genome sequence and gene models of Cq_real_v1.0 are available at the NCBI genome database with the BioProject number PRJNA394587. The high-throughput sequencing data for genome assembly, developmental transcriptome and EBC transcriptome analyses are available at Sequence Read Archive (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra</a>) under the BioProject number PRJNA394587, PRJNA394651 and PRJNA394652, respectively. The source code for HABOT2 is available at GitHub (<a href="https://github.com/asarum/HABOT2">https://github.com/asarum/HABOT2</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec22" data-title="Author Contributions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec22-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec22">Author Contributions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec22-content"><p>CZ, AC, LX, HMM, PA, MZ, WJ, PD, RH, XW and JB performed the experiments, CZ, GH, DL, DZ, FL and RL performed data analyses, HuiZ, SS, RH, JKZ and HZ designed the experiments and CZ, SS, RH, JKZ and HZ wrote the paper.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec23" data-title="Competing Financial Interests"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec23-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec23">Competing Financial Interests</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec23-content"><p>The authors declare no competing financial interests.</p></div></div></section>
